<div id="review_611920533" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/611920533" />
    <a title="Joey Gold" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19665343-joey-gold"><img alt="Joey Gold" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1366658329p2/19665343.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/611920533?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Gold" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Gold" href="/user/show/19665343-joey-gold">Joey Gold</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer611920533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14973697790209009437">"Go to heaven for the climate, hell for the company."<br/>-Mark Twain<br/><br/>"Ironweed" is chilly, bloody, thrilling, darkly-comic; and far from a crime novel. William Kennedy radiates those adjectives seemingly without any intention to do so. There aren't any mysteries, villains in a traditional sense (and DEFINITELY no heroes) and the action, even when it's colored in whiskey and guts, swarms on in a somewhat mellow, true-to-life pace, an understated rhythm that mimics earth's true tempo. A clock doesn't t</span>
  <span id="freeText14973697790209009437" style="display:none">"Go to heaven for the climate, hell for the company."<br />-Mark Twain<br /><br />"Ironweed" is chilly, bloody, thrilling, darkly-comic; and far from a crime novel. William Kennedy radiates those adjectives seemingly without any intention to do so. There aren't any mysteries, villains in a traditional sense (and DEFINITELY no heroes) and the action, even when it's colored in whiskey and guts, swarms on in a somewhat mellow, true-to-life pace, an understated rhythm that mimics earth's true tempo. A clock doesn't tick faster or slower, even when the most unusual person is wearing it, and Kennedy knows and supports this notion throughout the book. There aren’t really any key, life-changing moments in "Ironweed". At first glance this epiphany-free book might alienate readers. <br />Francis Phelan, the central character in this Great Depression tale, is a man scarred to the bone. When he looks back, a glance accompanied by somber binge-drinking, it seems that any place he roamed was a seedy war zone. The only redeeming past landscape, his glory days playing major league baseball, are colored now with "what could have been"-like questions. They are his passionate source of hope, his assurance that he is worthy of not being a brainless beast. <br />But do not expect reflective catharsis; for every inning there lies in his past a gory brawl (sometimes ending with a killing), a gruesome bender; utter ragged chaos. These reflections might cause a normal person gooseflesh; Phelan isn't one for too much remorse, not for his victims. Their fate, with the exception of his baby son he accidentally killed, does not trouble him. He himself does. He looks back in the third-person, dreaming of these bloody doings as if it were a movie. Then he realizes he is accountable. His hands are to blame, his animalistic soul. Slowly, this aging thug shakes hands with the skeletons in his closet. He takes the walk of shame back to his hometown of Albany, where his family and circle of former friends are located. <br />Helen, his "girlfriend" (as much as that is possible in boozy 1930's Bumland) is a tragic case. She apparently comes from a good background, and used to have true singing talent. She does not have the same ease and acceptance Francis has with their gritty, doomed lifestyles. She does not see it as inevitable destiny and is sanely sober enough to identify Francis' bad ways. It is not that he is in denial like many of the hilarious supporting characters (most of them, such as Rudy – Francis' workmate, are prone to illusions that society will claim them back), but he is less interested in redemption. She sings from time to time, even if it's in rowdy slums and wretched gin-mills, while Francis' revisits baseball with memory and photographs only. She tries to sleep indoors when possible (motels, friends and a local church) while he is adjusted to freezing weeds and torn car seats.<br />In the very first chapter we see Francis working as a gravedigger. The motif of ghosts, death and past-sins is the main one; however, many books dealing with these themes with sappy melodrama. Kennedy is smart in that he chose some of the most down-and-out misfits ever portrayed in fiction to address such topics. Because he has nothing much to lose, Francis Phelan's soul-searching is especially powerful. He curses. He will do anything for a dime. He is not prone to untruthful conclusions designed to give the reader comfortable closure. He scrimps enough money to buy a turkey for his estranged family; he shows a few baseball tricks to his niece; he arranges Helen human sleeping conditions before he has any night plans himself. He does learn to be good, in his way, but curiously these atonements ring true. No montages, sudden revelations and epiphanies. This is what makes "Ironweed" a unique work of turbulence. Kennedy is a cliché killer, his language is unusual and nearly poetic, and I recommend this book to anyone searching for a tale of short-cut free redemption. Or just looking for a very good book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14973697790209009437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2000512462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2000512462" />
    <a title="Martin Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53428220-martin-jones"><img alt="Martin Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1457285270p2/53428220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2000512462?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martin Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martin Jones" href="/user/show/53428220-martin-jones">Martin Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/33311763-ironweed">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2000512462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14812337371907128207">Ironweed Review<br/><br/>Ironweed is the story of Francis Phelan, a homeless man trying to survive on the streets of the American city of Albany during the Great Depression. Francis recalls Gerald, the baby son he accidentally dropped, who died of his injuries. Memories of three other men Francis killed during his life take on a physical reality. They come back as hallucinations to chat about life and matters arising. <br/><br/>A lot of the book consists of rambling conversations between Francis and other people ar</span>
  <span id="freeText14812337371907128207" style="display:none">Ironweed Review<br /><br />Ironweed is the story of Francis Phelan, a homeless man trying to survive on the streets of the American city of Albany during the Great Depression. Francis recalls Gerald, the baby son he accidentally dropped, who died of his injuries. Memories of three other men Francis killed during his life take on a physical reality. They come back as hallucinations to chat about life and matters arising. <br /><br />A lot of the book consists of rambling conversations between Francis and other people around him, whether they are visions of dead men, or the people with whom he shares the street. These discussions are by turns ridiculous and profound, funny and sad. At the end of the book, Francis finally talks about that crucial episode in his life when he dropped his son. But the significance of this confession is lost in the aimless talk around a fire as the homeless try to stay warm:<br />“Francis’ confession seemed wasted. Mentioning Gerald to strangers for the first time was a mistake because nobody took it seriously. And it did not diminish his own guilt but merely cheapened the utterance, made it as commonplace as Rudy’s brainless chatter about bears and wizards.”<br /><br />The whole book is contradictory in this way. Fantastical hallucinations exist alongside a relentlessly physical world where the reader wallows in the stink of poverty. Francis often portrays the most violent struggles of his life in terms of baseball, a game he once played professionally. Sometimes I found myself wondering if all his troubles were an illusion. Perhaps his grinding life on the street was actually a game. Maybe he has just made his life into a grim novel. Then just when I thought this added to the futility of Francis’s painful existence, I found myself looking at things from the other way round. Maybe the weight of disaster in Francis’ life isn’t as real as it seems. Anyone can make a small, insignificant mistake, which has dire consequences. It could happen to anyone.<br /><br />This is a hard book to enjoy, easier to admire.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14812337371907128207" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1968367456" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1968367456" />
    <a title="Kimberly Reads Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27489183-kimberly-reads-books"><img alt="Kimberly Reads Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603045680p2/27489183.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1968367456?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kimberly Reads Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kimberly Reads Books" href="/user/show/27489183-kimberly-reads-books">Kimberly Reads Books</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140070200" href="/book/show/589071.Ironweed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27489183-kimberly-reads-books?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27489183-kimberly-reads-books?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1968367456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5568939117802863637">Francis Phelan is one of those characters in literature that you really should dislike but you can't help feeling sympathy and compassion for him and his plight. He's simply a bum. He's spent his life running from his problems, but remains likable. He's taken lives, including his son's through an accident, he's shirked his responsibilities with his family by abandoning them, and he's ran away from his problems instead of facing them head on. He's exchanged all of that for a life of poverty, cold</span>
  <span id="freeText5568939117802863637" style="display:none">Francis Phelan is one of those characters in literature that you really should dislike but you can't help feeling sympathy and compassion for him and his plight. He's simply a bum. He's spent his life running from his problems, but remains likable. He's taken lives, including his son's through an accident, he's shirked his responsibilities with his family by abandoning them, and he's ran away from his problems instead of facing them head on. He's exchanged all of that for a life of poverty, cold, hunger, and loneliness. Through it all, we still see a side of him that is caring and generous towards his friends, Helen and Rudy, also hobos. We also see that he has no illusions as to his guilt and his place in how his life has turned out. He owns up to it, which is more than we can say for most people. This is a short but powerful look into a life damaged by guilt and remorse and the consequences of our actions and the desire to return home.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5568939117802863637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_152000804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152000804" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605586833p2/869064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152000804?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152000804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15938967440424907269">I usually love books about the down and out, and this book is full of some very haunted, guilty and broken people, but it all feels just a bit too melodramatic and humorless for its own good. And that isn't helped by Kennedy, who is a very gifted prose stylist, but just can't seem to settle on what style or perspective he's going to tackle these people's misery from. That kind of bouncing around isn't necessarily a bad thing (hell, Malcolm Lowry makes it work great), but it seems like he's just </span>
  <span id="freeText15938967440424907269" style="display:none">I usually love books about the down and out, and this book is full of some very haunted, guilty and broken people, but it all feels just a bit too melodramatic and humorless for its own good. And that isn't helped by Kennedy, who is a very gifted prose stylist, but just can't seem to settle on what style or perspective he's going to tackle these people's misery from. That kind of bouncing around isn't necessarily a bad thing (hell, Malcolm Lowry makes it work great), but it seems like he's just trying to attack everything from every direction at once, which is too bad because when he does linger long enough on a single point of view he creates some really beautiful passages. If you're desperate to find out what it feels like to be a destitute Irish American living in depression-era Albany, go for it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15938967440424907269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1740687806228921751">Stunning. Absorbing. Heartbreaking. Easily the best novel I've read in years. Each book in Kennedy's Albany Trilogy is worth your attention but the final novel, Ironweed, is the tour de force that will leave you at once thoughtful, enriched, and somber. Guess them Pulitzer Prize folks know their game, alright.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3200445656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3200445656" />
    <a title="Bob Schnell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16802193-bob-schnell"><img alt="Bob Schnell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359151411p2/16802193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3200445656?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Schnell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Schnell" href="/user/show/16802193-bob-schnell">Bob Schnell</a>
      </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3200445656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14259191771509215583">"Ironweed" by William Kennedy is like "Death of a Salesman" meets "Field of Dreams" as written by Charles Bukowski channeling John Steinbeck. I found it to be depressing yet funny and it even made me a bit ill in some parts. I really liked it.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('s942159nov+VsPAunuucm9EeKSahfmLbXwiiheDs1XvmROUuvnuVI9RWwm2T0OgprB1aI44bL2OYYknTJn1Z6w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

However, there is no doubt that The Old Wives\' Tale is a superb novel of its kind, and it is still as readable and enjoyable as ever. First published in 1908, it tells the story of the Baines sisters--shy, retiring Constance and defiant, romantic Sophia--over the course of nearly half a century. Bennett traces the lives of the sisters from childhood in their father\'s drapery shop in provincial Bursley, England, during the mid-Victorian era, through their married lives, to the modern industrial age, when they are reunited as old women. The setting moves from the Five Towns of the Staffordshire Potteries to exotic and cosmopolitan Paris. It was fascinating to learn from Bennett\'s journal how he saw an old lady in a cafe and was inspired to think of how her life might have been lived, how she must have once been young. The plot of the novel came to him fairly promptly, and, as they say, the rest is history. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15478784241329669493">William Joseph Kennedy is an American writer and journalist born and raised in Albany, New York. Many of his novels feature the interaction of members of the fictional Irish-American Phelan family, and make use of incidents of Albany's history and the supernatural. <br/><br/>Kennedy's works include <i>The Ink Truck</i> (1969), <i>Legs</i> (1975), <i>Billy Phelan's Greatest Game</i> (1978), <i>Ironweed</i> (1983, winner of 1984 Pulitze</span>
  <span id="freeText15478784241329669493" style="display:none">William Joseph Kennedy is an American writer and journalist born and raised in Albany, New York. Many of his novels feature the interaction of members of the fictional Irish-American Phelan family, and make use of incidents of Albany's history and the supernatural. <br /><br />Kennedy's works include <i>The Ink Truck</i> (1969), <i>Legs</i> (1975), <i>Billy Phelan's Greatest Game</i> (1978), <i>Ironweed</i> (1983, winner of 1984 Pulitzer Prize for Fiction; film, 1987), and <i>Roscoe</i> (2002).<br /><br /><br />See also <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Joseph_Kennedy">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_...</a></span>
